Melding his powers with the carving's magic, he directed his thoughts to the Talisman he'd instructed Grndel to give to Darkburst. A few moments later, he gasped as a scene shimmered into view. Darkburst was thrashing about in a water-filled tunnel, fighting for his life!
In different circumstances he would have enjoyed watching the badger drown, but if the young acolyte died before completing his task, Skelda's carefully laid plans would fail. Focusing his mind on the Dark Healing, Skelda quickly dropped into a trance, plunging down the First Path of Darkness. It was familiar ground, and he quickly reached the threshold of the Ninth Path. He willed his essence to conjugate with the Guardian of Blackness, grunting when the sentinel clawed itself deeper into the primitive areas of his mind. He pushed forward, knowing the gamble he was taking. If he failed to save his drowning son, all the planning and pain would have been for nothing.
*
The creature who'd started the chain of events leading to Darkburst's demise lay quietly, every sense alert, whiskers twitching nervously. Frightened by the sounds which had begun a few moments earlier, it dipped its head, whiskers brushing the floor. One ear carried a ragged notch and lay close to its head, the other rotated, searching out any danger. The sounds grew nearer, and the mouse scurried from its hiding place between the joints of the old field drain, running soundlessly over the rough surface away from the threat.
The noise startling the field mouse was reminiscent of dead leaves rustling in an autumn breeze. From a crack where one section of the concrete field-drain abutted the next, a spider appeared.
 - another popped into view.
 - and another.
 - more and more, until hundreds upon hundreds flowed from the joints, jerking this way and that, their small, yellow-tinged bodies joining into one undulating stream that oozed like pus from a gaping wound.
Dropping into the water, the spiders disappeared below the surface, a thick glutinous mass, each carrying its own tiny bubble of air trapped between the fine hairs on its body.
Darkburst's vision faded, and somewhere in the dim recesses of his mind, he knew he'd only a few moments left. His mind lost its sharpness to the reality of death, his thoughts meandering amongst happy cubhood memories; running along the moon-dappled pathways of Brockenhurst Forest, his sister chasing him; pouncing on an anthill, chasing the myriad small creatures as they flowed from their damaged nest; now his mother, a shimmering outline, smiling quietly.
Something touched Darkburst's snout, an irritation that made his skin pucker. Opening his eyes, he saw thousands of jostling, iridescent bubbles crowding in upon him. Had the Prime Mover come for him? Was this how life ended?
Urgent legs scurried across Darkburst's snout as spiders wove their webs, building a net of shimmering fibres around his mouth and nose. Darting forward, each spider unloaded its precious bubble into the net before returning to the surface to recharge. When their work was complete, they departed as silently as they'd come.
Air entered Darkburst's mouth and instinct took over. He gasped a deep shuddering breath, filling his lungs. Adrenaline pumped through his body, stirring his tired muscles into action. Wiping the remnants of the web from his snout, he struck out for the light at the end of the tunnel. Breaking free of the water, greedily sucking in air, he coughed repeatedly, purging his lungs. Weak though he was, he pulled himself from the water before collapsing onto the icy floor and passing out.
*
Darkburst didn't know how long he lay half-conscious on the dank surface, but when he finally found the strength to stagger out of the tunnel, the sun was high in the sky. Standing on the bank, he shouted across the river. There was no reply, just the urgent croak of a bullfrog hidden in some nearby rushes. He called until his voice cracked, and he could shout no longer. They had left him behind!
Hunting up some food, he ate, his body slumped in dejection. Tearing at the flesh of the scrawny squab he'd found under a nearby tree, he brooded on his misfortune. Why had they left him behind? He could think of no reason. Unless — he rose, the fur down his spine standing erect — unless Brock had persuaded his mother to leave him behind so he wasn't there to protect her. Brock would kill his mother, and there was no badger to stop him! His mind raced. Grindel had warned him about this. He shook his head, swallowing the bile rising in his throat, searching for an answer in the low, oppressive clouds. He must find his mother, and quickly!
*
Brock swallowed the last of the field mouse, his first meal in some time. Tossing a bone aside, he stretched and stood up. After cleaning his claws on a nearby tree, he went to check on Soffen. Quietly entering the temporary burrow, he leant over her, a fullness tightening his chest. She was still unconscious, but looking a little better. He nuzzled her neck, murmuring soft words of encouragement. "Have strength, my love. I'm here." Grooming her soft undercoat, he stroked her, as though handling a small cub. "I'm sorry I wasn't strong enough when you needed me," he whispered, voice cracking, "but I didn't understand. I didn't realise how much you blamed yourself for what's happened."
For two moons Brock kept a lonely vigil over Soffen, trickling water from his mouth into hers when she grew hot, piling dry leaves over her when she shivered. For two suns he'd lain by her side, not knowing whether she would live or die, cursing his impotence as she slipped ever deeper into a place he couldn't follow. But now he had a lighter heart, because she was showing signs of recovery; her gums no longer so pale, her breathing no longer so laboured. Brock settled beside her, his paw resting lightly against her flank, his thoughts turning idly to the events of the past few moons.
Broshee had gone off in search of the place where the stars fell to earth. She had some crazy idea that they would tell her where Boddaert's Magic lay hidden. She planned to use the magic to heal her mother, and all of his arguments had failed to deter her from making the journey. A headstrong youngster at the best of times, she'd refused to listen to him, determined to follow her own destiny. But for all his arguments, part of him was glad when she'd left. It gave him more time alone with Soffen.
As the sun prepared to welcome the moon, Brock half dozed, allowing disjointed memories to flow through his mind; a fire raging out of control; a long hard walk; his meeting with Grey; his first sight of Soffen — all jumbled together in a collage of colourful recollections. He'd made a lot of mistakes, it was all so obvious now. His responsibility for the deaths of his family and friends, for Grey and Darkburst — all these thoughts lay heavy on his mind. He should have died in the fire, but survived long enough to persuade Soffen to come on this fruitless search. And worst of all, he'd only done it to assuage his own feelings of culpability.
Laying his chin between his paws, Brock closed his eyes, coughing heavily, trying to loosen the tightness in his chest. A listlessness crept over him. Everything seemed to be too much trouble. Snuggling closer to Soffen's soft warmth, he fell into a fitful sleep.
*
Far away across Low Meadow, the sky grew darker and the stars brighter. Dwarfed by their magnitude, a frightened badger rested from her exertions, glancing about nervously, wondering what madness had brought her here. Gazing at the horizon, Broshee silently studied the stars, their brilliance reflecting in her eyes. She was much closer to them now and could clearly hear the strange noises they made calling back and forth to each other. Her gaze swept the trees standing in stark contrast against the sky, like slender sentries marching across the land. She shuddered; it was so cold and foreboding here, so different from anything she knew. The moonlight cast deep shadows, and as she hurried along, her mind turned to thoughts of her father.
Broshee had asked her mother about him many times, but Soffen always changed the subject. In the end, she stopped asking. But this didn't stop her from wondering. She knew her father would be a special badger, an extraordinary badger. No ordinary badger would have mated with a Healer. He'd have exceptional insight, be sensitive and strong — with a strength that nurtured rather than overpowered. Perhaps he was a Teller of The Way, like Brock, or even a Healer from another sett. She smiled when Brock's image entered her mind. She liked him; he was kind and patient, just as her father would be.
Still trying to piece together the enigma of her origins, Broshee continued picking her way across the desolate countryside, scrambling over the smaller rocks and skirting the larger ones. Her thoughts turned to her brother, and a quiet sadness filled her. Darkburst may have been moody and shy, but she'd loved him. The thought of her brother's one close friendship with the Preceptor, Grindel, made her shiver. That badger frightened her so much. Even his name sounded harsh. She began whispering it as she walked. A kind of chant. "Grindel — Grindel – Grindel." Growing tired of the game, she pushed the name from her mind, letting her thoughts wander back to her father. She smiled softly as a face came to her mind's-eye. It was a proud face, with bold black stripes down each cheek.
The distant echo of a dog's howl shattered Broshee's reflections. She froze, hackles rising. She'd never seen a dog, but like most young badgers, her parents warned her about their legendary ferocity and hunting skills. How often had she heard that familiar threat: 'If you don't go to sleep right now, I'll let the dogs come and get you.' She scented the air, her skin tingling when the dog howled again. It was a lot closer.
Broshee searched for somewhere to hide, pushing her way between two large rocks. She dug frantically at the hard ground, ignoring her bleeding pads as she clawed her way deeper into the stony soil. Muted at first, but growing louder, the soft clicking of claws on stone announced the dog's arrival. It passed close to her hiding place, and she pushed herself into the hole, holding her breath, willing the dog to go away. It stopped to sniff at the ground, growling deep in its throat; a harsh, rumbling sound.
The dog scented her, moving closer, sniffing between the rocks, barking excitedly when it smelt how near she was. The nearer the dog got, the more frantic it became, its digging interposed with high yaps. Earth and stones cascaded down on her, but she could do nothing, except bury her head in her flank and make herself as small as possible. She whimpered, fearful she was about to die an agonising death.

Chapter 14

Brock woke with a sore throat and a hacking cough. Staggering to his feet, he swayed. His head pounded, his sleep had done little to refresh him. He checked on Soffen, grunting his approval when he saw she was breathing much easier. Staggering his way out into the balmy air, he watched an owl searching for food. It swooped low over a nearby tussock, reminding him he hadn't eaten.
Scrabbling about at the base of a bush, he found a plump worm, but no sooner had he eaten it, he was violently sick. During a fit of coughing, he nearly choked on a thick yellow mucus filling his throat. Spitting out the lumps of phlegm, he missed the line of blood laced through the spittle.
Brock tried to order his thoughts. There was something nagging away at the back of his mind, something he had to do. Shaking his head, he struggled to remember what it was, but seemed incapable of coherent thought. He wandered in aimless circles, his brain on fire. Finally, he collapsed, his body shuddering as air whistled in and out of his mouth.
*
When he spotted the fallen tree laying across the river, Darkburst's spirits rose. Nearby was a large patch of trampled grass. He scented the ground, the short whiskers on his snout quivering with excitement. They had come this way, and judging by the pungency of the odour, not too long ago. Setting out again, he followed their trail, calling in the hope they would hear him.
As he wove his way in and out of the bulrushes edging the riverbank, his thoughts turned to the spiders that had saved his life. He could think of no other explanation than it was Grindel's work. That thought brought to mind the Talisman.
Brock groaned, opening his sticky eyes, running his tongue over the foul taste coating his teeth. Wrinkling his snout, he squeezed his eyes shut and tried to clear his head. Some badger had called his name. There it was again, faint and far away.
"Brock, mother, where are you? Can you hear me?"
Brock's mood lightened. It was Broshee come back. Everything would be alright now.
"Can you hear me? Where are you?"
The voice faded in and out, carried on the wind.
Brock tried to drag himself out of the shallow depression he lay in. "I'm here. Over here," he croaked.
The wind changed direction. The voice rose, then faded again. Brock groaned, swallowing painfully. It confused him. It sounded like Darkburst, but Darkburst was dead! The calling stopped, and he convinced himself he'd imagined it. Settling down again, he coughed, then collapsed back into a fitful sleep.
*
Darkburst strained to hear, but other than the echoes of his own shouts, heard nothing. Lowering his sensitive nose to the faint scent he'd been following, he set off at a fast trot. A short time later, the tracks freshened, and his hopes rose. His heart beat faster at the hope of soon of joining the others. He'd not admitted just how frightened and alone he felt. As he hurried along, he fretted about his mother and the danger she was in. Grindel's warning about Brock hung heavy on his mind. Without the Talisman for protection, she was in terrible danger. He stepped up his pace, thrusting his way through the undergrowth at a half-run.
Darkburst rounded a large rock, unaware that a creature watched his passing from the cover of a thick shrub, its bared teeth glistening in the fading light. The dog let the badger pass its hiding place, then set out after him, its paws making little noise on the crushed grass.
So single-minded was Darkburst in following the spoor, that he missed the telltale odour drifting on the wind. It was the low half-growl that warned him of the danger lurking behind. Pivoting, one paw half-raised, hardly able to breathe, he took in the terrifying sight. The dog, its head low over the patchy grass, curled its top lip back over long canines, studying him intently. Saliva tracked from the corner of the creature's mouth, and every muscle in its body quivered with tension. Darkburst backed away from the fearsome sight, spotting a long bloody rip  extending from its eye down the length of its snout. The dog followed him, step for step, the soft rumbling in its throat building into a menacing snarl.
Darkburst sensed the rock behind him far too late. The dog's swift advance had trapped him against the unyielding surface. The animal glared at him, its yellow-tinged eyes ablaze with hatred. Tearing his gaze from the terrifying stare, he glanced aside, seeking some avenue of escape. The dog took its chance, moving in for the kill.
In a frantic attempt at flight, Darkburst threw himself between the dog's legs, and heaved upwards. Thrown off balance, the creature yelped in surprise, staggering to one side. Seizing this slim chance, Darkburst slipped around the rock, running as fast as he could, knowing he stood little chance of outrunning the enraged creature. Crashing through the bulrushes, he ran hard, feet sucking noisily in the bank's mud. Behind him, the dog gained ground. Too terrified to look back, Darkburst pushed himself to his limit, heart beating so hard he felt the blood pounding through the large veins in his neck. Twice he stumbled and recovered.
The dog caught up with him as he scrambled his way up a sharp incline. It lunged at his hindquarters, grazing his skin. The sudden pain spurred him into an extra burst of energy. He jinked to one side, trying to throw the dog off course. It followed close on his heels, but slipped on a wet stone, giving Darkburst the chance he needed.
Topping the rise, he threw himself over the crest, rolling down the other side in a tangle of legs. Unable to gain his feet, he overshot the bank and dropped into the river's icy embrace with a loud splash. As the fast current bore him away, he fought to keep his head above the turbulent water. The dog, frustrated at his escape, ran to the water's edge, barking loudly in impotent fury.
Darkburst, whirled round by the river's fierce current, disappeared under the tempestuous surface four times before clawing his way back to the surface. Water soaked through the outer layer of his thick fur, saturating the soft undercoat, dragging him down into the dark depths of the river, his energy all but spent.
Deep along the stony riverbed, a fish appeared, its glinting eyes taking in the scene above. The thrashing limbs of the drowning creature did not frighten the fish. It swam closer, letting the tips of swirling fur caress its scaly back. Another fish darted forward, joining the first, then another. Few at first, they multiplied — ten, twenty, more and more, until the whole river writhed with their iridescent bodies. Collecting into a thick slithering mass, they rose as one, carrying Darkburst upwards, the water boiling with their urgent movements.
His head broke the surface, and he gasped long, shuddering breaths. Spotting a sandbank, he used the last of his strength to paddle onto the low ridge. Dragging himself clear of the water, he lay exhausted on the wet sand.
When he recovered, Darkburst struggled to his feet, studying the river with narrowed eyes. The fish had disappeared. He puzzled over the enigma. Twice now, creatures, that in normal circumstances would have shied away, had saved him. This had to be the work of Grindel.
*
Grindel cradled the dying badger's head, looking down into the glazed eyes with a hard, uncompromising stare. He'd been studying the carvings in the Taproot Chamber when the familiar tug cut through his thoughts. He'd resisted at first, annoyed that Skelda had chosen that moment to summon him — just when he was on the point of discovering what Darkburst had seen in the carvings. But Skelda's prodding insistence shattered the shimmering image. The Preceptor sighed, turning from the roots.
He arrived in time to witness the gathering of the fish, as Skelda, using the powers of the Dark Healing, saved the drowning badger. The vision of the river scene shimmering before his eyes brought the realisation of just how powerful the magic wielded by Skelda truly was, and it left him shaken.
Staring now into the deformed badger's eyes, Grindel saw their wild red spark diminishing. After saving Darkburst from drowning, Skelda had collapsed, falling to the floor as the Dark Healing finally extracted its ultimate penalty. Sensing the coming death of their benefactor, hundreds of tiny grubs dropped from his deformed head.
"You're dying, Skelda," Grindel whispered.
With much effort, Skelda nodded, his twisted body trembling as he fought the inevitable. "It's as it should be," he managed. "It's the price paid for using the Dark Healing."
The twisted old badger's trembling turned to spasmodic jerking, and Grindel leant closer, urgently shaking the dying boar. "Tell me," he shouted. "Tell me the secret. Tell me how to use the Dark Healing and I'll save you." Placing his ear against the twisted mouth, he strained to catch the dying words.
"Secret?" Skelda mumbled. "You wish to know the secret of the Dark Healing?"
Grindel's breath faltered. "Yes, yes, tell me."
Skelda's smiled and closed his eyes; a long sigh followed.
Grindel pounded the dying badger. "Tell me, you old fool. Tell me." Snout thrust right into Skelda's face, doing his best to ignore the stench, Grindel ground out words between clenched teeth. "Tell me how to use the Dark Healing!"
Skelda's mind was elsewhere now, floating on the Thirteenth Path of the Dark Healing, high above, near the roof of the chamber. Ignoring the exchange taking place below, the essence that was Skelda directed his consciousness outwards. Somewhere out there was the receptacle he needed, but he must find it quickly. Deep within the Darkness that was his anima, something flickered, stirring from the depths, and as the last threads of life drained from his body, Skelda discovered the way forward.
Grindel threw back his head, shrieking his frustration, tossing the useless body aside.
"I'll do it alone then, you old fool. I don't need you. I NEVER DID!" Stomping from the chamber, Grindel left behind the dead thing that had been such a dominating force in his life.
*
Broshee hummed to herself, tending her coat. There was a sadness to the sound. She'd always enjoyed the closeness grooming had brought between her and her mother; the idle chatter, the intimate touching, the lingering caresses. It was a part of her cubhood she found difficult to put aside.
Allowing her thoughts to drift back to her confrontation with the dog, Broshee realised how lucky she'd been. The animal, scrabbling at the earth between the rocks, dug into her hiding place. All she could do was push herself further into the shallow burrow, hoping that the dog would give up and go away, but the dog kept up its frantic activity until it finally made a space large enough to reach her. All too quickly, its powerful jaws were snapping dangerously close.
Broshee growled, striking out with her claws, ripping a long, jagged tear down the dog's snout. It yelped in pain and sprang backwards. The minor victory gave her the opportunity to launch a counterattack. Thrusting her rear feet against the packed earth, she hurled herself from between the stones, biting deeply into the dog's flank. It reared up, wary now Broshee had hurt it.
They circled each other, eyes locked, seeking an advantage.
Broshee sensed an open space behind and glanced over her shoulder, instantly realising her mistake. Taking her attention from the dog, even for a moment, had given it an advantage. It lunged forward, snapping at her neck, causing a painful wound. The dog snarled, not yet confident enough to follow through with its attack. Its hard yellow eyes watched her every movement. They moved back and forth, testing each other, looking for any weakness.
Warm blood trickled from the wound on Broshee's neck; she must finish this quickly. Sinking her claws into the earth, she turned sideways, scooping a pawful into its face. Her heart faltered when the animal howled, tossing its head violently to clear its eyes. The stinging dust had momentarily blinded it, and she took full advantage.
Running like the wind, heart pounding, breath pumping, feet barely touching the ground, Broshee ran for her life Onwards she ran; through ferns and thickets, across deep leaf mould and stony ground, beneath tall trees and high waving grass.
She finally collapsed when there was no need to run any further. She had left the dog far behind.
*
Shaking a light layer of moisture from her coat, Broshee stopped to rest. It was impossible to see far in the thick, early mist. She was afraid she might lose her way if she continued. Settling down, she ate the solitary worm she'd found earlier, but was still ravenous. The surrounding countryside was so desolate; flat and featureless.
She tried digging for food, without success. The black surface was too hard, refusing to yield to her tough claws. Here and there, an odd tuft of grass, or a tenacious spindly plant grew, but apart from those, even the vegetation had given up its unequal struggle. Running a claw tip over the strange-looking surface, she shuddered at its unnatural feel. Tipping her head, she listened to the star voices, feeling their movements beneath her feet.
*
Broshee stretched and stood up. The sun had finally burnt off the mist, but was now so hot the black ground hurt her feet. As she walked, her mind wandered, reliving past times spent in Brockenhurst Sett. She smiled, remembering the gossip she'd swapped with her friends. Climbing a gentle rise, she stumbled to a halt, her mouth falling open in amazement. Sweeping her gaze over the scene, she took a shuddering breath.
Too shocked to move, she stared, heart thumping in her chest. Below was a track, so wide she could barely see the other side. It was the widest track she'd ever seen, so wide she wondered if it really was a track. Hurtling along this were monsters that could only have come from her worst nightmares. Gross, overpowering creatures, smelling of burning bushes. Creatures blasting out great bouts of noise as they sped by, howling and screaming with voices that deafened her. Running on strange round paws that hummed on the hard surface, they chased each other with a breathtaking speed.
Broshee turned and fled, running mindlessly over the hard ground, petrified a monster would spot her before she got away. Tumbling back down the hillside, she collapsed at the bottom, her limbs trembling so violently, her teeth rattled. She glanced over her shoulder, her shudders easing when she saw no monster had followed. Still on the verge of panic, every muscle tense and ready to flee, she got to her feet. As time passed and no monster came, she relaxed. They hadn't seen her.
Broshee had spotted trees on the other side of the track, but were they the ones hiding Boddaert's Magic? If they did, she'd have to cross the track to reach them. That thought brought a new terror. With a dry mouth, she wondered what the monsters would do if she tried. Surely they would kill her?
The answer finally came, and she breathed a little easier. She would wait until the moon rose and stars appeared. They had called her here, so they would protect her.

Chapter 15

He mumbled and tutted and grumbled with every step. Mumbled, when a large stone got in his way and forced him to go around, tutted when his failing eyesight led him astray and he had to retrace his steps, but mainly he just grumbled. Grumbling was a way of life for Slikit, because he always found something to grumble about, even when things were going well.
As Slikit plodded along, the moonlight reflecting from his silver-grey fur made his jerky movements stand out in the darkness. He would have been hard put to tell you his exact age, he'd forgotten that long ago, but in his mind's eye he was still the lithesome young badger who'd bounded along forest tracks without a care in the world. But time had replaced his lithe, youthful body. Now older and slower, it often failed him when he needed it most. When had it happened? Where had he been when time had stolen it away?
Slikit had witnessed many things in his long life, among them the destruction of his home set in Spinningbrock; so many moons ago. Screaming yellow monsters scooped up the sett by the mouthful, spitting it out into the maws of other creatures that scurried away with their prize. Slikit could only suppose such a terrible thing was happening because the Custodians of Spinningbrock had forsaken the old ways, when younger badgers challenged their customs. He'd left the ruins of his sett on the edge of Veyatie Copse and never returned. Since then, he'd lived a solitary life, roaming Boddaert's Realm in a search of, he knew not what.
Slikit was thin, ribs clearly visible along his flanks, but it was a wiry thinness that belied surprising strength. His claws, now blunt and well worn, had lost the ability to regenerate themselves. His teeth too, so he found it hard to chew anything other than soft worms, grumbling heartily when cracking the shell of a beetle. He had lived alone for longer than he cared to remember, so when he stumbled across the scent of another badger, he was tempted to continue on his way. Something stopped him — a strangeness about the scent that invoked his curiosity. He followed it.
When Slikit eventually found the badger he was tracking, he knew he'd made the right decision. Shaking his head, he peered closely at the unconscious boar lying in the shallow hollow. "Oh, dear. This poor boar is ill." Swaying from foot to foot in a hiatus of indecision, he grumbled quietly to himself, wondering what to do, his nose jerking this way and that in an endless search for clues. "He just lies there, as though he's dead!"
Circling the badger, Slikit tutted angrily when a large thistle snagged his coat. "Are you alright badger?" he asked, prodding the prone figure then wrinkling his snout in consternation. Looking closer, Slikit saw the boar's chest rose and fell in a gentle rhythmic pattern. "Ah, then you're not dead," he muttered. "That's good, very good."
Prodding the boar with a determined poke, Slikit tried to wake him, but only covered his snout with a sticky yellow mucus that oozed from the boar's mouth. Wiping his snout on a tuft of grass, he tried again. "Come on, come on, you can't just lay there. Get up! Get up!" But the boar didn't respond. He paced back and forth. "Oh dear, he must be very ill indeed. What shall I do? He'll surely die if I don't help him."
Grumbling mightily to himself, Slikit looked longingly at the moon but found no inspiration. "In past times there would be a Healer to help in such a situation, indeed there would." The old boar quickly lost himself in one of his tangents, arguing the merits of the past compared to the present, regaling himself with memories of his cub-hood and better times.
The moon broke through the clouds and Slikit noticed a trail in the grass. It led into the undergrowth. "Ah, now, there's a clue," he muttered. Keeping his snout close to the ground, he followed the spoor, bumbling along as he contentedly grumbled at anything that got in his way.
Even though Slikit squinted his eyes to see better in the moonlight, he missed the dark shadow on the path. "Ouch! Who put that there?" Pulling his snout from a pile of earth, he snorted it clear. "Stupid badger! Stupid, stupid, badger!" he ranted, cocking his head as he studied the fresh mound. "But that's from a burrow, indeed it is. A badger's burrow."
Searching around, he found a small opening in the ground, an entrance to a temporary burrow. Pushing his head inside, he shouted, "Hello in there." There was no reply.
Slikit grumbled and fussed around the entrance for a time. Why didn't the badger answer? He could scent her clearly enough. Should he wait for an invitation or go in anyway? Growing impatient, he pushed into the tunnel and grumbled his way down into the interior. He immediately spotted the sow lying on a pile of dry leaves. "Well, this is a fine how-do-you-do. He's out there and you're in here, and when I called you, you didn't answer." The sow didn't respond, so he moved closer. "What's the matter with you? Are you ill as well, or is…?"
Slikit's grumbling faded away when he reached the sow's side and saw she was indeed unwell too. "Two," he grumbled, "bad enough one, but two?"
*
Broshee lay quietly, her mind in turmoil. She studied the moon, seeking reassurance from its pockmarked surface. Even though its bright countenance calmed her, she still jumped when a dog barked in the distance. She flicked an ear in alarm, wondering if it was the same dog which attacked her earlier. She shivered, reluctant to move, her courage deserting her now it was time to leave. The stars had appeared, but did not chase the monsters away. She could still hear their horrible calls on the far side of the hill.
The strange force that called her here nudged her again. Reluctantly rising to her feet, Broshee headed for the brow of the hill. Topping the rise, she looked down, heart pounding wildly. The monsters rushed along the track as before, but now they lit the track with light stolen from the moon! Mesmerised by the sight, drawn to the edge of the track by the compelling force that brought her here. The monsters ignored her, rushing on their way, buffeting her with their hot wake.
Closing her eyes, Broshee took a first tentative step onto the wide, black surface. The creatures did not attack her, just kept chasing each other, growling and howling their displeasure. Growing bolder, she locked her gaze on her target — a tall tree standing directly opposite. Setting out, she flinched when one monster passed right over her, its fiery breath scorching her fur.
As the terrified badger made her way across the smooth surface of the highway, the drivers — safe in their self-contained metal boxes — hurried about their business. If they noticed anything, it was a dark shadow on a darker surface, one of many such shadows littering the busy road that night.
*
"I did it. I said I could," Slikit collapsed in a heap. It had taken him most of the moon to drag the unconscious boar back to the burrow, and now his eyes twinkled accomplishment. "You really are lucky I happened along. Do you know that?" he told the unconscious boar. Mumbling contentedly to himself, he piled leaves in a corner, making a nest. "Without Slikit's help, you'd be dead. Do you know that?" Standing back to admire his work he nodded, then manoeuvred the sick boar onto the leaves, piling more on top.
"What's the matter? Is he hurt?" 
Slikit was so involved in the task that the voice startled him. He jumped, his heart beating wildly. "Dear oh dear, but you scared me female, you really did." Waddling across the chamber to where the sow lay, he squinted down at her. "You're awake then."
Soffen lifted her head, studying the small, bedraggled stranger with a critical stare. The effort proved too much, and she dropped her head back with a thump. "Tell me what's wrong with him."
Slikit tutted at the sow's impudence. "Where I come from females know their place, indeed they do," he muttered, wondering if he should leave right now and let her deal with the unconscious boar on her own.
Soffen struggled to her feet. Slikit, seeing how weak she was, piled leaves under her chest for support. She wrinkled her snout at him. "Thank you, badger. My name is Soffen."
"I'm Slikit," he answered self-consciously, his gaze on the floor.
Soffen nodded towards the badger Slikit had dragged back to the burrow. "That's Brock."
Slikit glanced at Soffen, then Brock.. "Well, whoever he is, he's very heavy." Nodding at the boar, he added, "I'm no Healer, but he seems pretty ill to me."
"Well, fortunately I am."
Slikit's face split into a huge grin. "You are?" he asked, worried he may have misheard. "A Healer? Really? Well, that's good news. Very good news indeed." Brushing some loose leaves from his sparse coat, he backed towards the entrance, making ready to leave. "Well, I'll be on my way then."
"No, you can't go. I'm much too weak to cope alone. You must stay, I need your help."
Slikit sighed heavily. Mumbling under his breath, he weighed up the options. He could stay and help, or he could go. But he could hardly leave them helpless. No, that wouldn't be right. Not right at all.
Soffen cut across his indecision. "Feel his nose Slikit. Tell me if it's hot."
Slikit cast Soffen a look. "I've already done that, female. Indeed, it was the first thing that I did."
"Well?" she queried. "Are you going to tell me, or just stand there like a lost toad?"
Slikit leant closer. "Female, I already know what's wrong with your friend." Furrowing his snout, he tilted his head quizzically. "But tell me, what has made you so ill, because it's obviously very different from what ails him."
Soffen's embarrassment was obvious. "It was something I ate, and none of your business. So, what do you think is wrong with him then?" she countered.
"From the yellow spittle dribbling from his mouth, and the difficulty he has breathing, I'd say he's got Yellow Cough. But then, I'm no Healer, am I?"
Soffen's breath caught. If the old badger was right… She shook her head in denial, her mouth suddenly dry. "The Yellow Cough? Are you sure?" When Slikit nodded, her heart sank. "But then you must leave at once!"
"Bah, what fear does the Yellow Cough hold for an old badger like me?" he reasoned. "If I catch it, I catch it. If I don't, I don't." From the moment he'd stumbled across the unconscious boar, he'd known he was suffering from Yellow Cough. The disease was not new to him.
Once, when just a cub, Yellow Cough spread its evil tentacles throughout his sett, killing most of the badgers living there, including his own family. It was a hard time for Slikit, and even now the memory brought a heavy sadness to his old heart. As usual, the illness disappeared as quickly as it came, leaving death and misery in its wake. 
Yellow Cough is a slow and painful death, feared by all badgers. The first sign, coughing up thick yellow pus, often laced with blood. Over the next few moons the sufferer lost weight, becoming weak and emaciated. Patches of fur fell out, leaving dreadful sores oozing a clear, sticky fluid. In the end stage, some hallucinated often convinced they were recovering, and feeling stronger, they might leave their chambers to wander in the forest, dying a lonely and painful death, far from loved ones.
Slikit smiled at Soffen. "No female, I have no fear of the Yellow Cough, indeed not."
Soffen shrugged wearily, suddenly looking exhausted and shrunken. "Then badger, you are a fool!"
Ignoring her jibe, Slikit picked up some leaves, cleaning the fur around Brock's mouth. His reply, when it finally came, was sharp and to the point. "That may be so female, but at least I don't eat things that violently disagree with me." He rubbed hard at the sick badger's neck, allowing time for his barb to strike home. "Not me," he finished with a final downward stroke.
Soffen ignored the old badger's remark, saying, "I need to collect some Mouse-ear. I can treat him with that. It's the only chance he has. I think I saw some… saw some… I think…"
Slikit turned when Soffen's voice faded. She had collapsed. Running over, he shook the Healer urgently, shouting in her ear. "What was that female? What did you say?" Tutting angrily when she failed to respond, he stood in the centre of the small chamber, snout wrinkled in agitation. "Something about a mouse's ears. The female said something about a mouse's ears. I'm sure of it." Shaking his head, he studied the floor. "That's strange. Strange indeed."
Slikit fussed about in the burrow for a time, moving things from here to there, and from there to here. He mumbled about the smallness of the chamber and grumbled about the smelliness of the leaves covering the floor. By the time he'd finished, the chamber was spick and span, and both badgers as comfortable as he could make them. Taking one last look around, he left.
Retracing his steps along the track, Slikit's snout twitched zealously. "It was somewhere along here," he assured himself, scenting the ground with his wrinkled old nose. "Somewhere along here." Pressing on, he jerked his head from side to side, trying to find the spoor he'd seen earlier. He hurried on, even though he knew it was hopeless. In his experience, any badger unlucky enough to catch Yellow Cough always died.
*
As Broshee crossed the track, the monsters roared and screamed their annoyance. Her eyes still stung from the dust and grit thrown up, and her ears rang from the terrible noise, but she was unharmed.
Standing at its base, Broshee looked up at the tree. She'd never seen such a smooth, straight trunk before, and its strangeness bothered her. She would have to climb it if she wanted to get Boddaert's Magic. Taking a deep breath, she reared up, flexed her claws, and began the long climb.
The tree exuded an unpleasant odour, stinging her eyes and nose. She pressed on, struggling upwards, with many stops to gather her strength. Her thoughts turned to her brother, the image of his strong, handsome face giving her the strength to continue.
After an arduous climb, Broshee finally reached a series of strange looking objects protruding from the side of the tree. Standing one above the other, they were cold to the touch, and the colour of shimmering starlight. Unable to decide what the bizarre objects were, she sat on the topmost one to rest. The view was disorientating and her senses spun. Closing her eyes, she clung on tightly, breathing deeply to calm herself. Recovered, she started out again, climbing higher and higher, afraid to look down because the height made her dizzy. Near the top, she smiled; her journey was nearing its end. Soon Boddaert's Magic would be hers.
Reaching the top of the tree, she gazed out over the land, so high now she experienced no sense of height. Even the monsters below looked small and less frightening. The trees stretched away on either side, two strange branches growing from each side of their trunks. No thicker than her leg, they dropped away in a gentle arc before rising to meet the next tree.
Broshee knew, of all the trees flanking the track, this was the tree; the resting place of Boddaert's Magic. This certainty was the reason she had come on such a dangerous journey, but searching the top of the tree, her heart sank when she found no sign of Boddaert's Magic. She'd been so certain, so sure she would find it here.
Tears of frustration filled her eyes. Now she would have to climb down and try the next tree. Wondering if she had the strength to undertake another climb, she turned to begin her descent, crying out in terror when she slipped, clutching wildly at a nearby branch. It embraced her in a harsh blue flickering light that singed her fur and convulsed her body. The smell of burning flesh assailed her sensitive nose, and the claws of her right paw, tightly clasped about the vibrating wire, popped and sizzled, welding themselves together in a smoking lump.
As the last vestiges of life fluttered weakly in Broshee's mind, a vision came to her; bright and clear. A vision of her father. Not the strong, handsome boar she had so often imagined. This badger was small and twisted, his body covered in bare patches and sores that wept a green pus. He smiled, holding out his paw. "Come, daughter," he beckoned. "It's truly time that we are one again."
Broshee twisted away in horror, dropping clear of the power lines, falling end over end. Time slowed, making the fall last forever. She felt at peace with herself, knowing life would go on without her, no longer frightened of death, just sad she would have no chance to dig her own chambers and fill them with the laughter of cubs. Darkburst's image filled her mind, and she knew she would soon be with him. Then her father's image superimposed itself over Darkburst. He beckoned again, but now with the face of Brock. Back and forth the images flowed. Brock, then Darkburst, then her father. Her father, then Darkburst, then Brock, until she could no longer tell them apart. "Father," she pleaded, "Help me. It hurts, it hurts so much"
After hitting the road, Broshee bounced once. Then, struck by a car, she flew high in the air, landing in a mud-filled ditch. Unable to move, she died a slow and lonely death, and as the last thoughts flickered and faded from her mind, tears filled her eyes, because there was no badger there to share her anguish and distress.
*
They glided in on the early morning light, black and shiny. One — two — then a group. Gathering at the unexpected prize, they fought, wings held high.
Broshee's body slowly disappeared until the only sign she had lived was the blood-red patch now staining the grass. As the last crow left, it circled the electricity pole twice, cawing its pleasure. The meal had been good; its stomach filled. Dipping low, it swooped over the road and headed off towards the tall trees of Migaro Lake.
With the bird went Broshee's claws, now fused into a star shape by the high voltage passing through them. It flew higher, gaining speed on the warm air currents rising from below. Primitive urges sped it on its way; to build a nest; to mate. Clutching its prize, the bird flew on its way, hoping the strange object it carried would attract a mate. Dipping its head, the crow glided across the treetops, searching for a nesting site.

Chapter 16

Grindel waited patiently until the noise died down before speaking. When he finally addressed the assembly on Fire Rock, his voice boomed out over them like thunder rumbling in the sky. "Badgers…" he paused until he had their full attention. "Friends, we gather this moon because I have grave news. News which affects each one of us." Nodding slowly he watched heads turn in a flurry of movement as badger looked at badger, wondering why they'd been summoned from their hunting. Grindel followed the ripples of motion through the crowd, as those at the front relayed his words to those further back. Finally, the gathering stilled and looked up at him expectantly.
Speaking slowly and clearly, pausing between each statement to add emphasis, he continued. "You know me, I am a fair badger. A badger who has only the good of the sett at heart. A badger who will do all in his power to protect both the sett and those living in it. It is because of this that I now ask you to trust me. To help me in what I must do." There were uneasy movements in the crowd, and a few shouts. "From this cycle I have dissolved the Council, and…" Drowned out by a sudden roar of protest, he waited for it to settle. "Yes, you heard right. I have disbanded the Council. In the circumstances, I had no choice." Sweeping the crowd with a hard stare, he twitched an ear. "I took these actions because there is treachery amongst the Custodians."
A gasp, as if from one throat, rose from the crowd.
Grindel nodded in agreement and continued. "A small group of badgers has acted directly against the Council. But worse than this, they have acted directly against the sett. Directly against you. THEY HAVE BETRAYED YOU!" The mountains amplified Grindel's shouts, throwing them back at the crowd in a confused conglomeration of echoes. Holding a paw aloft, he implored the throng to silence once more.
Rearing up on his hind legs, he reiterated his accusation, bellowing loudly, so that even those at the back could not mistake the scorn in his voice. He punched his words right at them, as though addressing each badger individually. "I have discovered treachery, right here in Brockenhurst Sett. In our own Council, this very moon." Pausing, he scanned the crowd again. "Treachery by the very Custodians elected to protect us!"
A voice from the back of the crowd broke the stunned silence. "What treachery?" it demanded.
Grindel swung his head in the questioner's direction, as though he could actually see the individual in the sea of faces. "You," he yelled, "You ask what treachery, and you deserve an answer." Pausing, he played the crowd, allowing them to become restless before tossing his next words at them, like sharp-edged stones. "Someone has stolen the Sacred Roots!"
With a quivering voice, as though he had difficulty containing his outrage, he glared down at them. "THE SACRED ROOTS HAVE GONE!"
Shouts of dismay rang out, and some pushed forward in their anger, crushing those at the front. Grindel urged the gathering nearer, which only added to the panic and injuries being suffered. The circle of guards forming a protective ring around him fought to restore order.
"Who has done this shameful thing?" the same voice shouted above the melee.
Grindel leant forward, glaring over the assembly. They fell silent. Raising his eyes to the moon, he stared at it, drawing out the crowd's tension. Hesitantly, as though reluctant to accuse another badger, he lowered his gaze and gave them the name they wanted. "It was the badger called Grey."
More and more voices took up the name until the air reverberated with it. "Grey! Grey! GREY!"
Grindel nodded and his guards moved forward to quieten the crowd, beating those that continued to shout. When order finally returned, he pointed to the circle of boars protecting him. "These badgers are replacing the Custodians. You will obey them." Holding a paw aloft to stem any protests, he smiled. "I assure you that as long as you do what you're asked, nothing will happen to you. It is only a temporary measure until I discover how deeply this treachery runs. Grey could not have done this alone. Others helped him."
Pointing into the sea of upturned muzzles, he narrowed his eyes. "Perhaps one of them stands next to you right now!" His word caused ripples through the ranks as badgers turned to their neighbour, suspicion in their stares. Pushing home his advantage, he pointed to the edge of the sett. "Soffen, our Healer, is one."
The crowd moaned in unison, as though struck by the guards. "And the stranger, Brock," Grindel continued. "The one who calls himself 'Teller of The Way'. He too is part of this plan to steal our most treasured possession." His next words whipped the crowd into uncontrolled hysteria. " What will you do, badgers? "WHAT WILL YOU DO WITH THE TRAITORS?"
From the rear of the crowd, came the answer Grindel sought. "Kill them. Kill the traitors!"
Grindel nodded, cocking an ear sky-wards. "What did you say?" he shouted back. "I can't hear you. What did you say?"
A roar rose, washing over the elated Preceptor. A roar, as from one throat. "KILL THEM! KILL THE TRAITORS!"
Grindel smiled over the baying crowd. This was far better than he dared hope. He had expected at least some resistance to his taking over the Council. When the throng settled down, he addressed them again. "If we hope to recover the Sacred Roots, we need a leader. A badger who is not afraid to face this danger head-on. A badger who has courage and foresight." Quickening the tempo, his voice grew. "A badger with the strength to fight the evil facing us." Rearing up and throwing back his head, he bellowed at the dark clouds. ""WHO SHALL THIS BADGER BE?"
The voice from the back of the crowd cried out once more. "The Preceptor shall be our leader. I say the Preceptor. The Preceptor will lead us."
The excited mob shouted Grindel's name, and it was a long time before he could make himself heard again.
*
Grindel smiled at the sombre badger. "You did well, my friend."
The badger lowered his gaze in respect, trying to hide his pleasure at such praise. It was as well not to give too much away.
Grindel sucked at a worm, then discarding the empty skin, he beckoned the badger nearer. "Tell me, Cherva, how goes it with the Custodians?"
Cherva moved his considerable bulk nearer to the Preceptor, feeling uneasy at such closeness. He was a large badger, muscular, his coat sleek and grey. His size cowed most badgers, but he knew that mere strength would be no match against this badger's cunning. The rumours were the Preceptor had magical powers.
Grindel had taken great pains in searching out a badger such as Cherva, travelling to many setts on the periphery of Brockenhurst Forest before finding a suitable candidate. He studied his choice, aware that the badger's hard, muscular body housed a cunning brain. True, the boar lacked the shrewdness that would make him a direct threat, but he would need careful watching.
"We imprisoned The Custodians as you directed," Cherva answered. "I have posted guards. They will not escape."
Grindel leant closer, smiling his satisfaction when Cherva's eyes widened slightly. "And Fenn, what about him?"
Cherva drew back a pace before answering. "I did as you instructed, Preceptor. I broke his jaw with a rock. He won't be talking to any badger for a long time. If ever."
Grindel nodded. "You may leave me now, but return when the moon is full. I want to speak with you again before I hold the next meeting. There are certain preparations we have to make."
*
Cherva backed from the chamber before hurrying away to inspect the Custodians' guard. He rechecked everything meticulously, knowing he would suffer horribly if things went awry with the Preceptor's plans. The knowledge he could have ripped the life from Grindel's body with a single slash of his claws made little difference to the awe he felt for his powers. He shuddered, recalling how he'd watched the slow death of a Custodian. Not that it upset him unduly, he'd killed himself. No, not the death, but the way it happened; killed by the Preceptor's magic.
The guards had dragged the Custodian forward, throwing him at Grindel's feet. The Preceptor glared down, eyes full of contempt. It was obvious the boar had suffered a beating. After struggling to his feet, he gave a contemptuous scowl. The Preceptor ordered the boar to leave Brockenhurst Valley, but the Custodian refused. Grindel did not argue, he simply cast a pawful of fine dust over the badger's head and uttered an incantation. The Custodian's body arched dramatically as he tore at his throat, mouth opening and closing, trying to breathe. Finally he collapsed to the floor and suffocated, terror bulging his rolling eyes.
Cherva didn't know what killed the badger — the dust, or his own terror — either way, with the pragmatism of a survivor, he immediately threw his strength behind the Preceptor, swearing his allegiance. On his new master's orders, he imprisoned the remaining Custodians in a damp chamber beneath Fire Rock.
*
Even outnumbered ten to one, Cherva had easily taken the Custodians during a meeting in the Grand Chamber, overcoming their weak resistance before they could organise themselves. They'd grown soft in their protected positions and were soon cowering before him.
Roughly pushing a guard aside when he failed to move quickly enough, Cherva stomped into the chamber containing the remaining Custodians. They lay about dejectedly on mouldy leaves, looking frightened and confused. As he entered, Cherva studied the straight walls and sharp angles. It was an unnatural space, oppressive and threatening. Quickly crossing to a badger laying in a corner, he yanked the Custodian's head to look him directly in his eyes. The boar whimpered quietly at the rough grasp on his broken jaw, his body trembling with pain.
Cherva noted the rebelliousness still lurking in the bright eyes and grunted. "Have your moment of defiance, fool," he growled. "You'll soon lose that sparkle of fight when the Preceptor has done with you."
Satisfied that all was as it should be, he called a guard, giving instructions that the prisoners were to receive water. There was no sense in letting them die too quickly.
As Cherva hurried along the now familiar tunnels leading to his sleeping chambers, he thought back over the past few moons. His life had transformed since coming to Brockenhurst Sett. After they stole the Sacred Roots from the Grand Chamber, the Preceptor visited him with talk of a diarchy and a promise of power. It was a persuasive declaration, hard to ignore. He was being offered the chance to be the first Suzerain of Brockenhurst Sett, with the authority to rule a realm within a realm. It was a dream he'd held since cubhood. Position and power.
After the Preceptor's visit, Cherva quickly set about collecting a band of trainee guards, only choosing the largest and most aggressive boars; those holding grudges against their fellow badgers. Taking them away from the main sett, he ordered a temporary burrow dug in Low Meadow. It was damp and dismal, just as he knew it would be; discomfort would lend a sharpness to the training.
Cherva trained the badgers hard, never letting up on the pressure. It took a great of effort to train the boars in overcoming their natural aversion to killing, but they slowly succumbed. It was necessary if the Preceptor's plans were to work. After two new moons, he'd done the best he could. They were now a team who worked well together, could disable and maim, would kill on his orders, and showed no mercy to themselves or others. With these badgers Cherva would hold sway over the whole sett, second only to the Preceptor himself.
Promising the guards' rewards of the best sleeping chambers and choice of females, he left to report back to Grindel, grinning broadly as he considered his new position.
*
Grindel smiled at the progress Cherva had made. Now he had total control of Brockenhurst Sett. The first phase of his plan, to fortify the sett against attack, was well under way. The major defence was earthworks to guard the sett entrance. It would let defenders protect the sett and confuse attackers with a maze. Supporting this would be Cherva's trained fighters.
Guards patrolled the paths leading into Brockenhurst Forest, and any travellers found, put to work on the complicated earthworks at the entrance. The sett entrances were under constant watch, badgers only being allowed out under strict supervision, to collect food or work on the defences. The guards dealt with dissent, both publicly and harshly.
Grindel wallowed in the atmosphere of fear that settled over Brockenhurst Valley. Using Cherva's terror tactics, he'd strengthened his position so much that badgers now cast their eyes to the ground when he passed. No longer under the hated Skelda's power, he was free to pursue his own plans. This gave him time to explore Skelda's lair. These singular burrows and chambers made him uneasy. Pushing the fears aside, he became fascinated by the bizarre configurations they took. One chamber especially intrigued him, and he spent many moons there, trying to unlock its secrets.
The walls of the chamber were too smooth for dust to settle on, rising higher than his eyes could follow. Far above, lost in a murky darkness, he sensed a roof spanning the astonishing area. The chamber was oppressive; the smallest noise, even his own soft breathing, echoing back from every direction. When he first entered, he looked about uneasily, expecting the roof to come crashing down on him. A deep layer of undisturbed dust covered the floor. It was obvious no creature had crossed its surface in many moons. Creeping across the vast emptiness, he reached the far side to discover a large object towering over him. The sides grainy, the edges sharply angled, its shape reflected the chamber in which it lay. He walked around it, finding its unidentifiable scent, sharp and musty. He licked it, surprised it was a ligneous substance. This comforted him, because he understood trees. Strange black markings covered the larger surface, vaguely reminiscent of the carvings in the Taproot Chamber.
Shortly after the discovery, Grindel had the first dream; a dream that recurred over the following seasons; a dream that became an obsession, consuming his wake-time as it did his sleep. In the dream he entered the vast chamber and stood before the object. A power radiated from it, an almost palpable, physical thing, that drew him closer — as though the moon, trapped inside, was trying to break out, oozing bands of bright, cold light through the surface. Setting to work with his powerful jaws, he found little difficulty in chewing into it. Spitting out splinters, he kept going until he broke through into the interior. It was here the dream always ended. Frustration at not seeing what was inside cut like a spume of fire. It felt as though the answers to all the questions he could ever ask were just out of reach; tantalisingly close, but still unobtainable. He became convinced the dream was a vision sent by the dead Skelda. It would explain why such power radiated from it. It was the 'Fire Soul', spoken of in the Legend. His best course of action was to leave it undisturbed until he could bring the other parts to the chamber and make Boddaert's body whole. Until then, he ordered the entrance blocked and instructed Cherva to kill the badgers who undertook the work.
*
Grindel stood on the ramparts of the giant earthworks, surveying his domain with a broad smile, savouring the absolute power that would soon be his. When Darkburst returned with the missing elements of Boddaert's Magic, he would put his plan into action.
There was hardly any room on top of Fire Rock when Grindel called his next meeting. They waited for the Preceptor to appear, fidgeting and murmuring in subdued voices. Flanked on all sides by the guards that were so prevalent of late. Most kept their gaze lowered, having learnt a challenging stare was answered with a stinging blow or a bruising bite. Grindel mounted the platform that Cherva's guards had constructed and waited for the crowd to quieten.
When Grindel had their full attention, his powerful oratory rang out over their heads. "Badgers, friends, I have information you should know. News of those who stole the Sacred Roots. The low-bellied cowards who sneaked away with our oldest and most treasured artefacts." He paused, giving them time to take in his words. One or two badgers whispered excitedly, but quickly settled down when a guard approached. "Now I've heard questions being asked by many of you, and you're right to ask them. But remember the reasons for your present discomfort, and the hard work you're asked to endure. Does any badger doubt I have their best interests at heart?"
Watching the crowd, Grindel noted those who might cause him trouble in the future; those with hardened stares and questioning eyes. Smiling, he continued. "When I ordered the building of the earthworks, it was for a reason." He nodded again with more significance, wrinkling his snout as though sharing their doubts. "Not because I wanted it, but because of the dangers posed by Grey and his cohorts. At substantial risk to myself, I have discovered why they stole the Sacred Roots in such a cowardly way. Yes, badgers. I, your Preceptor, risked my life discovering the answer."
Grindel's next pause proved too much. The crowd pushed forward expectantly, eager to hear what he had to say. "These cowards took the Sacred Roots to search for Boddaert's Magic." Shouting above the clamour this statement caused, he berated the crowd, building their hysteria. "Having stolen this treasure, what will they do with it when they return?" Massaging the crowd's fear, he glared, picking out individuals, making them part of the theft. "I'll tell you what. They'll use it to destroy us!" Shouting louder above the reaction of the hysterical crowd, his voice drowned even the most boisterous cries. "They'll use Boddaert's Magic to destroy Brockenhurst Sett, and that includes every badger here this moon. You and your cubs!"
The tightly packed mass swayed back and forth as Grindel's words struck home. "And I for one am determined that won't happen. I will not stand by and do nothing while they ravage our sett, our dreams, our very lives." He brought the mob to fever pitch, rising on his hind legs, voice rising another notch. "Are you with me, badgers?" Eyes dancing with excitement, spittle flying from his lips, he continued his tirade. "Are you strong, my brothers?"
"Yes," the boars shouted ecstatically.
"Are you strong, my sisters?"
"Yes," the sows screamed, some almost fainting with excitement.
"Will you stand by my side and fight this evil?"
The badgers crammed on Fire Rock that fateful moon, galvanised by Grindel's oration, erupted with one voice. Spurred on by the rising fever gripping every one, they screamed their allegiance, no longer despondent and distrustful, fired now by a common purpose.
Grindel threw back his head, his voice shaking with elation. "Will you fight with me, my badgers?"
"Yes!" they roared, overwhelmed by their own emotions.
"I need volunteers," Grindel cried. "Volunteers to finish building the fortifications. Volunteers to gather food for the workers and guards. Volunteers to watch the pathways. Will you volunteer, my badgers?"
As one, the crowd surged forward, urged on by the guards at the rear. Shoving excitedly, ignoring the terrified screams of those being trampled at the front, pushing closer in their search for a saviour, urging Grindel to wash them clean, give them the strength to fight the coming evil. Above them stood their master; tall, regal, beaming, waiting to give Cherva a signal.
At the Preceptor's nod, Cherva rose to his full height, his powerful body dwarfing those around him. "Guards," he bawled above the melee, "bring me the traitor. The one called Fenn."
Heads turned, and the throng immediately took up the Custodian's name. "Fenn! Fenn! Fenn!"
They dragged the terrified badger before the mob, and the shouting swiftly built to a roar. Clearing a space in front of the staging, the guards threw Fenn to the ground. Grindel stared down at the hapless Custodian, nodding contentedly as he studied the boar's swollen jaw. "Well badger, what have you to say in your defence?" he demanded. "What have you to tell us about your part in the plot to destroy Brockenhurst Sett?"
Fenn looked about helplessly, unable to speak, scarcely able to breathe through his swollen snout.
"I'm waiting, badger," Grindel threatened, his voice vibrating with indignation that the Custodian refused to answer. The hapless Fenn shook his head miserably, gazing at the crowd with fearful eyes, finding only enmity in his search for a friendly face. Grindel's voice lashed down again. "Do you deny you're part of this evil plot to take over Brockenhurst Sett and enslave every badger living here? Do you deny you helped Grey steal our beloved Sacred Roots, killing two Custodians in the process?" This revelation brought a gasp of horror from the crowd and they surged nearer Fenn, threatening him with flashing claws and sharp teeth. The guards hurriedly pushed them away.
"Well?" Grindel demanded. Fenn could only continue staring at the crowd, unable to answer. Grindel's next words came loaded with a flat menace. "If you don't answer me badger, I shall have no option but to take your silence as an admission of your guilt." Fenn tried desperately to answer his tormentor, but the pain proved too much and he collapsed. Grindel pointed down at the prone figure, a look of triumph glowing in his eyes. "See, my badgers?" he crowed in delight. "Do you see the guilt? The traitor Fenn is so overwhelmed with remorse that he can't face you. He's fainted. A coward and a traitor." Rearing up again, he solicited the crowd's approval. "What do you say, my brothers? Shall he die? SHALL HE DIE?"
"Yes!" howled the mob in unison.

Chapter 17

Slikit lay on his back, legs splayed awkwardly, chest rising and falling as he tried to recover his breath. It had been a long, hard chase, but he'd succeeded. He'd left the temporary burrow, determined to get the ears needed by the Healer, and there lay the prize by his side.
Slikit's poor eyesight made catching the creature difficult, but his old nose did not let him down. The mouse, though quick, had made the mistake of diving headlong for cover. Had it stayed in the open, it would have seen another moon, but even Slikit's blunt claws had no difficulty digging it from its ill-chosen hiding place. Moaning softly, he rolled over and stood up, favouring his aching muscles. Bending over the mouse, he deftly nipped the ears off, frowning at the notch torn into one.
Slikit ate little now — his appetite lessening as he aged — so he left the mutilated body of the mouse for some other creature to discover and made his way back to the burrow. The mouse ears, clamped tightly between his teeth, kept him from his favourite pastime, but he made up for this by grumbling to himself in his imagination. On the trek back to the burrow, he thought about the two badgers waiting for him — one sick with the yellow fever, the other sick with a very different illness. The Healer held powerful feelings for her companion, and he pondered how she'd accidentally eaten something that so violently disagreed with her. He smiled, recalling the way she'd looked at the sick boar with such obvious affection.
Sighing, Slikit pushed such thoughts from his mind, afraid they would stir too many memories of happier times. Fussing his way back into the burrow, he worked his way down into the chamber, greeting Soffen with a nod, glad to see she was awake and looking much better. Unfortunately, the same wasn't true for Brock, who lay half-curled on his bed of leaves, panting.
Crossing to Soffen. he spat the ears onto the floor. "Here you are, female. I had some trouble finding these for you, but I managed."
Soffen glanced down at the two bedraggled ears, then back up at Slikit. For a moment she didn't react, then a sparkle entered her eyes. Shaking her head, she burst into laughter, looking at Slikit helplessly as her body shook.
Slikit frowned in anger. Having done as she'd asked, why was she laughing at him It was humiliating!
Soffen finally found her voice. "I'm so sorry, Slikit," she gasped. "I don't mean to offend you. But it's so f-f-funny!" Turning away, she hid her snout in her flank, still giggling.
"What are you laughing at?" he demanded.
Pushing at the ears with a paw, she tried to maintain her dignity. "When I said Mouse-ear I meant the plant, not real ones!"
A heavy silence descended on the burrow. Soffen held her breath, trying to keep a serious expression, afraid she might offend the old badger even more. But Slikit joined in with the Healer's giggles and pretty soon he too was gasping for breath. It'd been too long since he'd laughed like this; such helpless, merry laughter that purified the soul and lifted the spirits.
"You didn't mean a mouse's ears, you m-m-meant mouse ears," he spluttered. "And I chased that mouse all over the place. F-f-from here to there, and b-back again!" It took a while before either could look at the other without bursting into a fit of the giggles, but at length their laughter subsided and both looked away in embarrassment.
Checking on Brock, Soffen asked Slikit to help her collect the proper Mouse-ear. Outside the moon was bright, the clouds sparse, with a light wind blowing across the low hills. Their laughter had cleared the air between them, and both now felt easier in the other's company. They walked side-by-side, lost to their innermost thoughts, savouring the mood. Since leaving his sett all those seasons ago, Slikit had spent so little time in the company of other badgers that he always felt awkward, never knowing what to say or how to behave. Now that he wanted to talk to Soffen, he was finding it difficult.
After a couple of false starts, he finally asked a question. "Where are you from, Soffen?"
She took so long answering that Slikit began wondering if she'd heard his question."I'm from Brockenhurst Sett. Have you heard of it?"
Slikit nodded. "But of course. Hasn't every badger?" They walked on in silence for a while, then at length he tried again. "So what brings you all the way here from Brockenhurst? It's a long way, quite a journey, in fact."
"We came searching for Boddaert's Magic," Soffen answered quietly.
Slikit stumbled to a stop, staring at her. Surely he'd misheard? She stared back with an equal intensity.
"What's wrong?" she asked, wrinkling her snout.
Slikit licked dry lips. "You said you came searching for Boddaert's Magic? The ancient magic?"
"Yes, Slikit. We came searching for the ancient magic."
"But that's just a myth, a story!"
"No, it's true," she assured him. "I'm a Healer, and Brock's a Teller. Who better to know that the Legend is fact?"
Slikit mulled over what she'd said. "And the two of you came searching for this myth?"
"Five," Soffen corrected. "There were five of us." 
"There are five of you? But where are the others? Did they go back for help?"
"Two of them died," she answered, softly. "Grey, a Custodian, and Darkburst, one of my cubs." After a slight pause. "Broshee, my other cub has disappeared. I don't know where she is."
"Two dead?" Slikit murmured.
"Yes, both drowned." Another pause. "Oh, my poor cub." The last word came as a sob.
"And your other cub? Also missing?"
A large tear ran down Soffen's snout. She shook it away with a quick flick of her head. When she finally spoke again, her voice was full of despair. "Yes. When I woke up, she'd gone. Did you notice any signs of her when you found me?"
Deep inside, she already knew the answer. Broshee was dead! The emptiness she felt inside told her that. She hadn't asked before because she was afraid of the answer. Suddenly everything became too much, and she collapsed onto the grass, sobbing quietly.
As Soffen cried for her cubs, Slikit stood at her side in embarrassed silence. Twice he extended a paw, as if to comfort her, but held back. He finally withdrew, sitting a short distance off, studying the moon as if it was the most important thing in his life. Let her suffer her grief in privacy. That would be best.
Soffen slowly gathered her emotions. Casting Slikit a hesitant look, she half-smiled. "Come on," she said in a quiet voice, "let's find that Mouse-ear."
Soffen pushed the hurt and guilt into the deepest recesses of her mind. It had been such self-indulgent feelings that had brought about her attempt on her own life, leading to Brock's grave illness. If she'd been there to help him, how different things might have been. Stay positive, don't give in to despair. Pushing such thoughts aside, she hurried on with the search, leaving Slikit to bumble along behind as best he could.
It was some time later she looked across at the old boar "You know, I think Broshee may have gone to fetch help. I think she went back to Brockenhurst Sett. Yes, that's what she'd do."
"Yes, I'm sure you're right," Slikit agreed.
They fell silent again, continuing their search, pushing through the thick undergrowth. Now and then Soffen stopped to examine a plant before rejecting it with an impatient shake of her head. Slikit could contain himself no longer.. "Soffen, what did you hope to do with Boddaert's Magic when you found it?"
Soffen stopped for a moment, scratching her flank. "Brock saw a vision about the destruction of Brockenhurst Sett."
"And you hoped to use Boddaert's Magic to save the sett?" Slikit prompted.
Soffen nodded. "The vision wasn't too clear about how to do that. But yes, that was our hope."
Slikit thought this over for a moment. "But how could you find Boddaert's Magic? Where would you begin?"
"We were hoping to find that answer on the Sacred Roots."
Slikit's eyes widened in horror, his mind thrown into turmoil. If they had the Sacred Roots, they could only have stolen them. The Preceptor of Brockenhurst Sett would never let such precious items out of his protection. He was an old fool, helping thieves!
"Come on, this way," Soffen said, heading off at a tangent, worrying she'd said too much.
Slikit's slow movements reflected his reluctance to follow the Healer as she led him through a tangled patch of undergrowth. They stopped at a bush. "Here's some. Come and look, so you'll know what it is in the future."
Slikit wandered over, studying the plant. The leaves were small, with sparse hairs on top and white down underneath. Its pale yellow flowers had a red tinge. "Is this mouse's ears?" he asked, his mind still struggling with the idea of her stealing the Sacred Roots.
A soft smile formed fleetingly on Soffen's lips. "Yes, that's Mouse-ear," she replied. "And all we need now is a special fungus that usually grows in the same place."
Gathering a selection of leaves from the plant, Soffen quickly located the fungus she needed and headed back to the burrow. Slikit trailed along behind, doing his best to hide his agitation as he deliberated on what he was getting involved in.
*
By the time Soffen and Slikit arrived back at the burrow, the sky had deepened to a bright, blood red. The air, crisp and clear, carried the scent of rain. With the prospect of a shower at any moment, they hurried down to the chamber, glad to be undercover.
When Soffen saw how much worse Brock was, concern clouded her face. His breathing was so laboured that every lungful shook his entire body. She prepared the plant and fungus, laboriously chewing them into a sticky paste, which she carefully dribbled into his mouth. Finished, she looked across at Slikit, tears shining in her eyes.
"He's dying," she whispered. "He's dying and there's nothing I can do about it. The only chance of saving him is for me to get him back to Brockenhurst Sett. I've got a potion there that might work. Will you help me get him there?"
The old badger exhaled heavily as he rose, weary and depressed. "Yes Soffen," he agreed, "I'll help you." Reluctant though he was to accompany Soffen and Brock to Brockenhurst Sett, he felt obligated. Having stopped to help, he was loath to back out. And anyway, he could slip away quietly when the time was right. There would be plenty of opportunities.
The febrifuge Soffen had given Brock began its work, and he had soon recovered enough to sit up. Looking about in confusion, he smiled at Soffen. "You're better," he said throatily.
Soffen drank in every word, hungry to hear him.
Brock looked about, wrinkling his snout as though trying to remember something important. "Broshee has gone to look for the stars," he suddenly blurted.
Soffen's eyes widened in shock. "She's alive? Broshee is alive?" She could scarcely believe it. "But I thought…" Then shaking her head, she asked, "Stars? What stars?"
Taking his time, Brock told her about the place where the stars rushed along beneath the sky, following a long line of trees. How they howled and moaned at each other as they went, making the earth tremble with their anger. As Brock told his story, Slikit stayed in the background, growing more taciturn now there was another badger to deal with. After Brock finished, Soffen explained they should return to Brockenhurst Sett as quickly as possible.
It elated Soffen to hear Broshee was still alive, but waiting for her return before starting the long journey back meant Brock would be too ill to travel. She'd leave a sign, telling her where they'd gone. As they prepared to leave, Brock suddenly clasped a paw to his head, crying out in pain.
Soffen's eyes widened, and she rushed to his side. "What is it? What's the matter?"
"A pain," Brock mumbled through clenched teeth. "A terrible pain." He rocked back and forth. "What? What did you say?"
Soffen shook her head. "Nothing, Brock. I said nothing."
Brock staggered backwards, eyes rolling wildly. "But I can hear voices," he cried, pounding at his head with a shaking paw. "In here, I can hear voices."
Slikit mumbled something under his breath, backing away, wary at the way Brock was behaving.
Brock grimaced again, then suddenly relaxed a little. "It's alright. I think it's easing now. Yes, it's definitely going away." He took a tentative breath, ready to stop the moment he felt the pain again.
Soffen wrinkled her snout, smiling uneasily at him. "Are you sure?"
Brock nodded slowly. "Yes. I just feel weak, that's all."
"Help me, Slikit," Soffen ordered. When he hung back, she frowned. She tried to reassure the old badger. "It's alright, he's fine now. Really, it's only a reaction to the potion I've given him." But she knew it wasn't, or the result of the illness he had. Something else was at work; something beyond her understanding.
*
The trio stopped for a rest beside a small pool of water. As Soffen ate the worms she'd gathered, she watched Brock thoughtfully from beneath hooded eyes. There was something disquieting about his behaviour. It reminded her of another badger, but she couldn't think who. Since leaving the burrow, Brock constantly mumbled to himself, answering unasked questions, as though talking to another badger.
Rested, the group pushed on, both Soffen and Slikit taking turns to help the struggling badger. When he collapsed from exhaustion, they took another break. He lay on the ground, sides heaving, struggling for breath. Turning away, Soffen forced herself to think about what she'd need to do once they arrived back. Dig up the piece of bark in which she'd placed her strongest potion. She'd hidden it after her father's death, cursing it for the pain it brought. He'd prepared it using the Dark Healing, draining the last of his strength.
